expected.</div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Kenya</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'k\' in \'kind\'">k</span><span title="/&#603;/: \'e\' in \'dress\'">&#603;</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="/j/: \'y\' in \'yes\'">j</span><span title="/&#601;/: \'a\' in \'about\'">&#601;</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <small>locally&#160;</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[&#712;k&#603;&#626;a]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kenya_pronunciation.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/91/Kenya_pronunciation.ogg" title="Kenya pronunciation.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>), officially the <b>Republic of Kenya</b> (<a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a>: <i lang="sw">Jamhuri ya Kenya</i>), is a country in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> with  47 semiautonomous <a href="/wiki/Counties_of_Kenya" title="Counties of Kenya">counties</a> governed by elected <a href="/wiki/Governor" title="Governor">governors</a>. At 580,367 square kilometres (224,081&#160;sq&#160;mi), Kenya is the world\'s 48th largest country by total area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> With a population of more than 52.2 million people, Kenya is the  27th most populous country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Kenya\'s capital and largest city is <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a> while its oldest city and first capital is the coastal city of  <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kisumu" title="Kisumu">Kisumu City</a> is the third largest city and a critical inland port at Lake Victoria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Other important urban centres include <a href="/wiki/Nakuru" title="Nakuru">Nakuru</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eldoret" title="Eldoret">Eldoret</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilotic</a>-speaking <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoralists</a> (ancestral to Kenya\'s Nilotic speakers) started migrating from present-day Southern Sudan into Kenya around 500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EhretCHS_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-EhretCHS-14">[14]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_colonization" title="European colonization">European colonisation</a> of Kenya began in the 19th century during the <a href="/wiki/European_exploration_of_Africa" title="European exploration of Africa">European exploration of the interior</a>. The modern-day Kenya emerged from <a href="/wiki/East_Africa_Protectorate" title="East Africa Protectorate">a protectorate</a> established by the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> in 1895 and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Kenya_Colony" title="Kenya Colony">Kenya Colony</a>, which began in 1920. Numerous disputes between Great Britain and the colony led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_uprising" title="Mau Mau uprising">Mau Mau revolution</a>, which began in 1952, and the subsequent declaration of independence in 1963. After independence, Kenya remained a member of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Kenya" title="Constitution of Kenya">current constitution</a> was adopted in 2010 to replace the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Kenya_(1963)" title="Constitution of Kenya (1963)">1963 independence constitution</a>.\n</p><p>Kenya is a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democratic</a> republic, in which elected officials represent the people and the president is the head of state and government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Kenya is a member of <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_Market_for_Eastern_and_Southern_Africa" title="Common Market for Eastern and Southern Africa">COMESA</a>, and other international organisations. With a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gross_National_Income" title="Gross National Income">GNI</a> of 1,460,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Kenya is a lower-middle-income economy. <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Kenya" title="Economy of Kenya">Kenya\'s economy</a> is the largest in eastern and central Africa,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-18">[18]</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a> serving as a major regional commercial hub.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-18">[18]</a></sup> Agriculture is the largest sector; tea and coffee are traditional cash crops, while fresh flowers are a fast-growing export. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Service_industry" title="Service industry">service industry</a> is also a major economic driver, particularly tourism. Kenya is a member of the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a> trade bloc, though some international trade organisations categorise it as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greater_Horn_of_Africa" title="Greater Horn of Africa">Greater Horn of Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Africa is Kenya\'s largest export market, followed by the European Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toctext">Women</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#Education"><span class="tocnumber">6.7</span> <span class="toctext">Education</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-43"><a href="#Media"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Media</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-44"><a href="#Literature"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Literature</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-45"><a href="#Music"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Music</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-46"><a href="#Sports"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Sports</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-47"><a href="#Cuisine"><span class="tocnumber">7.5</span> <span class="toctext">Cuisine</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-48"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-49"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-50"><a href="#Sources"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Sources</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-51"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>\n<p>The Republic of Kenya is named after Mount Kenya. The earliest recorded version of the modern name was written by German explorer <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ludwig_Krapf" title="Johann Ludwig Krapf">Johann Ludwig Krapf</a> in the 19th century. While travelling with a Kamba caravan led by the legendary long distance trader Chief Kivoi, Krapf spotted the mountain peak and asked what it was called. Kivoi told him "<i>K&#297;-Nyaa</i>" or "<i>K&#297;&#297;ma- K&#297;&#297;nyaa</i>" probably because the pattern of black rock and white snow on its peaks reminded them of the feathers of the cock ostrich.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sullivan_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-sullivan-21">[21]</a></sup> The Agikuyu, who inhabit the slopes of Mt. Kenya, call it K&#297;r&#297;ma K&#297;r&#297;nyaga in <a href="/wiki/Kikuyu_language" title="Kikuyu language">Kikuyu</a>, while the Embu call it "Kirenyaa." All three names have the same meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ludwig Krapf recorded the name as both <i>Kenia</i> and <i>Kegnia</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krapf_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krapf-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krapf_452_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-krapf_452-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foottit_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-foottit-25">[25]</a></sup> Others say that this was&#8212;on the contrary&#8212;a very precise notation of a correct African pronunciation <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'k\' in \'kind\'">k</span><span title="/&#603;/: \'e\' in \'dress\'">&#603;</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="/j/: \'y\' in \'yes\'">j</span><span title="/&#601;/: \'a\' in \'about\'">&#601;</span></span>/</a></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> An 1882 map drawn by Joseph Thompsons, a Scottish geologist and naturalist, indicated Mt. Kenya as Mt. Kenia, 1862.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sullivan_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-sullivan-21">[21]</a></sup> The mountain\'s name was accepted, <a href="/wiki/Pars_pro_toto" title="Pars pro toto">pars pro toto</a>, as the name of the country. It did not come into widespread official use during the early colonial period, when the country was instead referred to as the <a href="/wiki/East_Africa_Protectorate" title="East Africa Protectorate">East African Protectorate</a>. It was changed to the <a href="/wiki/Kenya_Colony" title="Kenya Colony">Colony of Kenya</a> in 1920.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Kenya" title="History of Kenya">History of Kenya</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Turkana_Boy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1437" data-file-width="504" decoding="async" height="399" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Turkana_Boy.jpg/140px-Turkana_Boy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Turkana_Boy.jpg/210px-Turkana_Boy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Turkana_Boy.jpg/280px-Turkana_Boy.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turkana_Boy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkana_boy" title="Turkana boy">Turkana boy</a>, a 1.6-million-year-old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominid" title="Hominid">hominid</a> fossil belonging to <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Fossils found in Kenya suggest that primates roamed the area more than 20 million years ago. Recent findings near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Turkana" title="Lake Turkana">Lake Turkana</a> indicate that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominid" title="Hominid">hominids</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_habilis" title="Homo habilis">Homo habilis</a></i> (1.8 and 2.5 million years ago) and <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> (1.9 million to 350,000 years ago) are possible direct ancestors of modern <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i>, and lived in Kenya in the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> epoch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Isaac_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Isaac-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During excavations at Lake Turkana in 1984, paleoanthropologist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Leakey" title="Richard Leakey">Richard Leakey</a> assisted by <a href="/wiki/Kamoya_Kimeu" title="Kamoya Kimeu">Kamoya Kimeu</a> discovered the <a href="/wiki/Turkana_Boy" title="Turkana Boy">Turkana Boy</a>, a 1.6-million-year-old fossil belonging to <i>Homo erectus</i>. Previous research on early hominids is particularly identified with <a href="/wiki/Mary_Leakey" title="Mary Leakey">Mary Leakey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louis_Leakey" title="Louis Leakey">Louis Leakey</a>, who were responsible for the preliminary archaeological research at <a href="/wiki/Olorgesailie" title="Olorgesailie">Olorgesailie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hyrax_Hill" title="Hyrax Hill">Hyrax Hill</a>. Later work at the former site was undertaken by <a href="/wiki/Glynn_Isaac" title="Glynn Isaac">Glynn Isaac</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Isaac_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Isaac-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neolithic">Neolithic</span></h3>\n<p>The first inhabitants of present-day Kenya were <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> groups, akin to the modern <a href="/wiki/Khoisan" title="Khoisan">Khoisan</a> speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EhretC18_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-EhretC18-28">[28]</a></sup> These people were later replaced by agropastoralist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cushitic" title="Cushitic">Cushitic</a> speakers from the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EhretHRSC_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-EhretHRSC-29">[29]</a></sup> During the early <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a>, the regional climate shifted from dry to wetter climatic conditions, providing an opportunity for the development of cultural traditions, such as agriculture and <a href="/wiki/Herding" title="Herding">herding</a>, in a more favourable environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EhretC18_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-EhretC18-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Around 500 BC, <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilotic</a>-speaking <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoralists</a> (ancestral to Kenya\'s Nilotic speakers) started migrating from present-day Southern Sudan into Kenya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EhretCHS_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-EhretCHS-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmbroseALR_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmbroseALR-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmbroseSG_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmbroseSG-31">[31]</a></sup> Nilotic groups in Kenya include the <a href="/wiki/Samburu_people" title="Samburu people">Samburu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luo_(family_of_ethnic_groups)" title="Luo (family of ethnic groups)">Luo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkana_people" title="Turkana people">Turkana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maasai_people" title="Maasai people">Maasai</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ILO_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-ILO-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the first millennium AD, <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a>-speaking farmers had moved into the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EhretACA_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-EhretACA-33">[33]</a></sup> The Bantus originated in West Africa along the <a href="/wiki/Benue_River" title="Benue River">Benue River</a> in what is now eastern <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> and western <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WayneSmith_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-WayneSmith-34">[34]</a></sup> The Bantu migration brought new developments in agriculture and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_ferrous_metallurgy" title="History of ferrous metallurgy">iron working</a> to the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WayneSmith_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-WayneSmith-34">[34]</a></sup> Bantu groups in Kenya include the <a href="/wiki/Kikuyu_people" title="Kikuyu people">Kikuyu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luhya_people" title="Luhya people">Luhya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kamba_people" title="Kamba people">Kamba</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gusii_people" title="Gusii people">Kisii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meru_people" title="Meru people">Meru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuria_people" title="Kuria people">Kuria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aembu" title="Aembu">Aembu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambeere" title="Ambeere">Ambeere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wadawida" title="Wadawida">Wadawida</a>-Watuweta, Wapokomo and <a href="/wiki/Mijikenda_peoples" title="Mijikenda peoples">Mijikenda</a> among others.\n</p><p>Remarkable prehistoric sites in the interior of Kenya include the archaeoastronomical site <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namoratunga" title="Namoratunga">Namoratunga</a> on the west side of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Turkana" title="Lake Turkana">Lake Turkana</a> and the walled settlement of ThimLich Ohinga in <a href="/wiki/Migori_County" title="Migori County">Migori County</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Swahili_culture_and_trade_.281st_century.E2.80.9319th_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Swahili_culture_and_trade_(1st_century&#8211;19th_century)">Swahili culture and trade (1st century&#8211;19th century)</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Swahili_culture" title="Swahili culture">Swahili culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Zanzibar" title="Sultanate of Zanzibar">Sultanate of Zanzibar</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lamu_door.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2012" data-file-width="1444" decoding="async" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lamu_door.jpg/220px-Lamu_door.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lamu_door.jpg/330px-Lamu_door.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lamu_door.jpg/440px-Lamu_door.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lamu_door.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A traditional <a href="/wiki/Swahili_culture" title="Swahili culture">Swahili</a> carved wooden door in <a href="/wiki/Lamu" title="Lamu">Lamu</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Kenyan coast had served host to communities of <a href="/wiki/Ironwork" title="Ironwork">ironworkers</a> and communities of Bantu subsistence farmers, hunters and fishers who supported the economy with agriculture, fishing, metal production and trade with foreign countries. These communities formed the earliest city states in the region which were collectively known as <a href="/wiki/Azania" title="Azania">Azania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the 1st century CE, many of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City-states" title="City-states">city-states</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> began to establish trade relations with <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>. This led to the increase economic growth of the Swahili states, introduction of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> influences on the Swahili <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bantu_language" title="Bantu language">Bantu language</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_diffusion" title="Cultural diffusion">cultural diffusion</a>, as well as the Swahili city-states becoming a member of a larger trade network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nanjira_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nanjira-37">[37]</a></sup> Many historians had long believed that the city states were established by Arab or Persian traders, but archeological evidence has led scholars to recognize the city states as an indigenous development which, though subjected to foreign influence due to trade, retained a Bantu cultural core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Sultanate" title="Kilwa Sultanate">Kilwa Sultanate</a> was a medieval <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate" title="Sultanate">sultanate</a>, centred at <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Kisiwani" title="Kilwa Kisiwani">Kilwa</a> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>. At its height, its authority stretched over the entire length of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swahili_Coast" title="Swahili Coast">Swahili Coast</a>, including Kenya. It was said to be founded in the 10th century by <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_al-Hassan_Shirazi" title="Ali ibn al-Hassan Shirazi">Ali ibn al-Hassan Shirazi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> Sultan from <a href="/wiki/Shiraz" title="Shiraz">Shiraz</a> in southern Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> However, scholars have suggested that claims of Arab or Persian origin of city-states were attempts by the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili</a> to  legitimize themselves both locally and internationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Since the 10 century, rulers of Kilwa would go on to build elaborate coral mosques and introduce copper coinage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_Museum_Kilwa_pot_sherds.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1651" data-file-width="2576" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/British_Museum_Kilwa_pot_sherds.jpg/220px-British_Museum_Kilwa_pot_sherds.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/British_Museum_Kilwa_pot_sherds.jpg/330px-British_Museum_Kilwa_pot_sherds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/British_Museum_Kilwa_pot_sherds.jpg/440px-British_Museum_Kilwa_pot_sherds.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_Museum_Kilwa_pot_sherds.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pottery sherds from the <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Sultanate" title="Kilwa Sultanate">Kilwa Sultanate</a>, founded in the 10th century by the <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> Sultan <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_al-Hassan_Shirazi" title="Ali ibn al-Hassan Shirazi">Ali ibn al-Hassan Shirazi</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Swahili built Mombasa into a major port city and established trade links with other nearby city-states, as well as commercial centres in Persia, Arabia, and even India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> By the 15th-century, Portuguese voyager <a href="/wiki/Duarte_Barbosa" title="Duarte Barbosa">Duarte Barbosa</a> claimed that "Mombasa is a place of great traffic and has a good harbour in which there are always moored small craft of many kinds and also great ships, both of which are bound from Sofala and others which come from Cambay and Melinde and others which sail to the island of Zanzibar."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Later on in the 17th century, once the Swahili coast was conquered and came under direct rule of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omani_Arabs" title="Omani Arabs">Omani Arabs</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">slave trade</a> was expanded by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omani_Arabs" title="Omani Arabs">Omani Arabs</a> to meet the demands of plantations in <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Initially these traders came mainly from Oman, but later many came from Zanzibar (such as <a href="/wiki/Tippu_Tip" title="Tippu Tip">Tippu Tip</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>  In addition, the Portuguese started buying slaves from the Omani and Zanzibari traders in response to the interruption of the transatlantic slave trade by British abolitionists.\n</p><p>Swahili, a Bantu language with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, and other Middle Eastern and South Asian <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a>, later developed as a <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> for trade between the different peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-35">[35]</a></sup> Swahili now also has loan words from English.\n</p><p>Throughout the centuries, the Kenyan Coast has played host to many merchants and explorers. Among the cities that line the Kenyan coast is the City of Malindi. It has remained an important Swahili settlement since the 14th century and once rivalled Mombasa for dominance in the African Great Lakes region. Malindi has traditionally been a friendly port city for foreign powers. In 1414, the Chinese trader and explorer <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> representing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a> visited the East African coast  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treasure_voyages" title="Treasure voyages">on one of his last \'treasure voyages\'</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Malindi authorities welcomed the Portuguese explorer <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a> in 1498.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="British_Kenya_.281888.E2.80.931962.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Kenya_(1888&#8211;1962)">British Kenya (1888&#8211;1962)</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kenya_Colony" title="Kenya Colony">Kenya Colony</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Africa_1909_16a.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="679" data-file-width="610" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Africa_1909_16a.png/220px-Africa_1909_16a.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Africa_1909_16a.png/330px-Africa_1909_16a.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Africa_1909_16a.png/440px-Africa_1909_16a.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Africa_1909_16a.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_East_Africa" title="British East Africa">British East Africa</a> in 1909</div></div></div>\n<p>The colonial history of Kenya dates from the establishment of a <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German</a> protectorate over the Sultan of <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>\'s coastal possessions in 1885, followed by the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_British_East_Africa_Company" title="Imperial British East Africa Company">Imperial British East Africa Company</a> in 1888. Imperial rivalry was prevented when Germany handed its coastal holdings to Britain in 1890. This was followed by the building of the <a href="/wiki/Uganda_Railway" title="Uganda Railway">Kenya&#8211;Uganda railway</a> passing through the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop&#230;dia_Britannica_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop&#230;dia_Britannica-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The building of the railway was resisted by some ethnic groups&#8212;notably the <a href="/wiki/Nandi_people" title="Nandi people">Nandi</a> led by <i><a href="/wiki/Orkoiyot" title="Orkoiyot">Orkoiyot</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Koitalel_Arap_Samoei" title="Koitalel Arap Samoei">Koitalel Arap Samoei</a> for ten years from 1890 to 1900&#8212;however the British eventually built the railway. The <i>Nandi</i> were the first ethnic group to be put in a native reserve to stop them from disrupting the building of the railway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop&#230;dia_Britannica_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop&#230;dia_Britannica-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the railway construction era, there was a significant inflow of Indian people, who provided the bulk of the skilled manpower required for construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-50">[50]</a></sup> They and most of their descendants later remained in Kenya and formed the core of several distinct Indian communities such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ismailism" title="Ismailism">Ismaili Muslim</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sikh" title="Sikh">Sikh</a> communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While building the railway through <a href="/wiki/Tsavo" title="Tsavo">Tsavo</a>, a number of the Indian railway workers and local African labourers were attacked by two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lions" title="Lions">lions</a> known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tsavo_maneaters" title="Tsavo maneaters">Tsavo maneaters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the outbreak of World War I in August 1914, the governors of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_East_Africa" title="British East Africa">British East Africa</a> (as the protectorate was generally known) and <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a> agreed a truce in an attempt to keep the young colonies out of direct hostilities. <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Lettow-Vorbeck" title="Paul von Lettow-Vorbeck">Lt. Col. Paul von Lettow-Vorbeck</a> took command of the German military forces, determined to tie down as many British resources as possible. Completely cut off from <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Lettow-Vorbeck" title="Paul von Lettow-Vorbeck">von Lettow</a> conducted an effective <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">guerrilla warfare</a> campaign, living off the land, capturing British supplies, and remaining undefeated. He eventually surrendered in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Rhodesia" title="Northern Rhodesia">Northern Rhodesia</a> (today Zambia) fourteen days after the Armistice was signed in 1918.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="339" data-file-width="821" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg/300px-Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg/450px-Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg/600px-Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Uganda_Railway" title="Uganda Railway">Kenya&#8211;Uganda Railway</a> near <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>, about 1899.</div></div></div>\n<p>To chase von Lettow, the British deployed the <a href="/wiki/British_Indian_Army" title="British Indian Army">British Indian Army</a> troops from <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">India</a> but needed large numbers of porters to overcome the formidable logistics of transporting supplies far into the interior on foot. The <a href="/wiki/Carrier_Corps" title="Carrier Corps">Carrier Corps</a> was formed and ultimately mobilised over 400,000 Africans, contributing to their long-term politicisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1920, the East Africa Protectorate was turned into a colony and renamed Kenya for its highest mountain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop&#230;dia_Britannica_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop&#230;dia_Britannica-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the early part of the 20th century, the interior central highlands were settled by British and other European farmers, who became wealthy farming coffee and tea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> (One depiction of this period of change from one colonist\'s perspective is found in the memoir <i><a href="/wiki/Out_of_Africa" title="Out of Africa">Out of Africa</a></i> by Danish author Baroness Karen von Blixen-Finecke, published in 1937.) By the 1930s, approximately 30,000 white settlers lived in the area and gained a political voice because of their contribution to the market economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The central highlands were already home to over a million members of the <a href="/wiki/Kikuyu_people" title="Kikuyu people">Kikuyu</a> people, most of whom had no land claims in European terms and lived as itinerant farmers. To protect their interests, the settlers banned the growing of coffee, introduced a hut tax, and the landless were granted less and less land in exchange for their labour. A massive exodus to the cities ensued as their ability to provide a living from the land dwindled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_50-4"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-50">[50]</a></sup> There were 80,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whites_in_Kenya" title="Whites in Kenya">white settlers</a> living in Kenya in the 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Throughout <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya_in_World_War_II" title="Kenya in World War II">Kenya was</a> an important source of manpower and agriculture for the United Kingdom. Kenya itself was the site of <a href="/wiki/East_African_Campaign_(World_War_II)" title="East African Campaign (World War II)">fighting</a> between Allied forces and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Italian</a> troops in 1940&#8211;41 when Italian forces invaded. <a href="/wiki/Wajir" title="Wajir">Wajir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a> were bombed as well.\n</p><p>In 1952, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Princess Elizabeth</a> and her husband <a href="/wiki/Prince_Philip,_Duke_of_Edinburgh" title="Prince Philip, Duke of Edinburgh">Prince Philip</a> were on holiday at the <a href="/wiki/Treetops_Hotel" title="Treetops Hotel">Treetops Hotel</a> in Kenya when her father, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_George_VI" title="King George VI">King George VI</a>, died in his sleep. The young princess cut short her trip and returned home immediately to take her throne. She was crowned Queen Elizabeth II at Westminster Abbey in 1953 and as British hunter and conservationist <a href="/wiki/Jim_Corbett" title="Jim Corbett">Jim Corbett</a> (who accompanied the royal couple) put it, she went up a tree in Africa a princess and came down a queen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mau_Mau_Uprising">Mau Mau Uprising</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_Uprising" title="Mau Mau Uprising">Mau Mau Uprising</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi,_Kenya.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2176" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg/220px-Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg/440px-Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi,_Kenya.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A statue of <a href="/wiki/Dedan_Kimathi" title="Dedan Kimathi">Dedan Kimathi</a>, a Kenyan rebel leader with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mau_Mau" title="Mau Mau">Mau Mau</a> who fought against the British colonial system in the 1950s.</div></div></div>\n<p>From October 1952 to December 1959, Kenya was in a state of emergency arising from the <a href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_Uprising" title="Mau Mau Uprising">Mau Mau rebellion</a> against British rule. The Mau Mau, also known as the Kenya Land and Freedom Army, were primarily members of the Kikuyu Group.\n</p><p>The governor requested and obtained British and African troops, including the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_African_Rifles" title="King\'s African Rifles">King\'s African Rifles</a>. The British began <a href="/wiki/Counter-insurgency" title="Counter-insurgency">counter-insurgency</a> operations. In May 1953, General Sir <a href="/wiki/George_Erskine" title="George Erskine">George Erskine</a> took charge as commander-in-chief of the colony\'s armed forces, with the personal backing of <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wunyabari_O._Maloba_1993_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wunyabari_O._Maloba_1993-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The capture of War&#361;hi&#361; Itote (also known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_China" title="General China">General China</a>) on 15 January 1954 and the subsequent interrogation led to a better understanding of the Mau Mau command structure. <a href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_Uprising#British_gain_the_initiative" title="Mau Mau Uprising">Operation Anvil</a> opened on 24 April 1954, after weeks of planning by the army with the approval of the War Council. The operation effectively placed Nairobi under military siege. Nairobi\'s occupants were screened and the Mau Mau supporters moved to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detention_camp" title="Detention camp">detention camps</a>. The Home Guard formed the core of the government\'s strategy as it was composed of loyalist Africans, not foreign forces such as the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> and King\'s African Rifles. By the end of the emergency, the Home Guard had killed 4,686 Mau Mau, amounting to 42% of the total insurgents.\n</p><p>The capture of <a href="/wiki/Dedan_Kimathi" title="Dedan Kimathi">Dedan Kimathi</a> on 21 October 1956 in <a href="/wiki/Nyeri" title="Nyeri">Nyeri</a> signified the ultimate defeat of the Mau Mau and essentially ended the military offensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wunyabari_O._Maloba_1993_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wunyabari_O._Maloba_1993-56">[56]</a></sup> During this period, substantial governmental changes to land tenure occurred. The most important of these was the <a href="/wiki/Swynnerton_Plan" title="Swynnerton Plan">Swynnerton Plan</a>, which was used to both reward loyalists and punish Mau Mau.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence">Independence</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="366" data-file-width="255" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg/170px-Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c2/Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first President and founding father of Kenya, <a href="/wiki/Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Jomo Kenyatta">Jomo Kenyatta</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The first direct elections for native Kenyans to the Legislative Council took place in 1957. Despite British hopes of handing power to "moderate" local rivals, it was the <a href="/wiki/Kenya_African_National_Union" title="Kenya African National Union">Kenya African National Union</a> (KANU) of <a href="/wiki/Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Jomo Kenyatta">Jomo Kenyatta</a> that formed a government. The Colony of Kenya and the Protectorate of Kenya each came to an end on 12 December 1963 with independence being conferred on all of Kenya. The United Kingdom ceded sovereignty over the Colony of Kenya. The Sultan of Zanzibar agreed that simultaneous with independence for the Colony of Kenya, the Sultan would cease to have sovereignty over the Protectorate of Kenya so that all of Kenya would be one sovereign, independent state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenneth_Roberts-Wray_1966._P._762_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kenneth_Roberts-Wray_1966._P._762-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> In this way, Kenya became an independent country under the Kenya Independence Act 1963 of the United Kingdom. Exactly 12 months later on 12 December 1964, Kenya became a republic under the name "Republic of Kenya".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenneth_Roberts-Wray_1966._P._762_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kenneth_Roberts-Wray_1966._P._762-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Concurrently, the Kenyan army fought the <a href="/wiki/Shifta_War" title="Shifta War">Shifta War</a> against ethnic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somali_people" title="Somali people">Somali</a> rebels inhabiting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Frontier_District" title="Northern Frontier District">Northern Frontier District</a>, who wanted to join their kin in the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Republic" title="Somali Republic">Somali Republic</a> to the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> A cease fire was eventually reached with the signature of the Arusha Memorandum in October 1967, but relative insecurity prevailed through 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hogg1986_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hogg1986-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howell1968_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howell1968-61">[61]</a></sup> To discourage further invasions, Kenya signed a defence pact with <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> in 1969, which is still in effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_first_president_of_Kenya">The first president of Kenya</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Presidency of Jomo Kenyatta">Presidency of Jomo Kenyatta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Jomo Kenyatta">Jomo Kenyatta</a></div>\n<p>On 12 December 1964 the Republic of Kenya was proclaimed, and <a href="/wiki/Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Jomo Kenyatta">Jomo Kenyatta</a> became Kenya\'s first president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Under Kenyatta, corruption became widespread throughout the government, civil service, and business community. Kenyatta and his family were tied up with this corruption as they enriched themselves through the mass purchase of property after 1963.Their acquisitions in the Central, Rift Valley, and Coast Provinces aroused great anger among landless Kenyans. His family used his presidential position to circumvent legal or administrative obstacles to acquiring property. The Kenyatta family also heavily invested in the coastal hotel business, with Kenyatta personally owning the Leonard Beach Hotel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He ruled until his death in 22 August 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moi_era">Moi era</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Daniel_arap_Moi" title="Daniel arap Moi">Daniel arap Moi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Daniel_Moi" title="Presidency of Daniel Moi">Presidency of Daniel Moi</a>, <a href="/wiki/1978_Kenyan_presidential_election" title="1978 Kenyan presidential election">1978 Kenyan presidential election</a>, <a href="/wiki/1988_Kenyan_general_election" title="1988 Kenyan general election">1988 Kenyan general election</a>, and <a href="/wiki/1992_Kenyan_general_election" title="1992 Kenyan general election">1992 Kenyan general election</a></div>\n<p>Moi was Kenya\'s president from 1978 to 2002. At Kenyatta\'s death in 1978, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_arap_Moi" title="Daniel arap Moi">Daniel arap Moi</a> became President. Daniel arap Moi retained the Presidency, being unopposed in elections held in 1979, 1983 (<a href="/wiki/Snap_election" title="Snap election">snap elections</a>) and 1988, all of which were held under the single party constitution. The 1983 elections were held a year early, and were a direct result of <a href="/wiki/1982_Kenyan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="1982 Kenyan coup d\'&#233;tat attempt">an abortive military coup attempt</a> on 2 August 1982.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moi_and_Bush.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="221" data-file-width="298" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Moi_and_Bush.jpg/220px-Moi_and_Bush.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/24/Moi_and_Bush.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moi_and_Bush.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Daniel_arap_Moi" title="Daniel arap Moi">Daniel arap Moi</a>, Kenya\'s second President, and George W. Bush, 2001</div></div></div>\n<p>The abortive coup was masterminded by a low ranked Air Force serviceman, Senior Private <a href="/wiki/Hezekiah_Ochuka" title="Hezekiah Ochuka">Hezekiah Ochuka</a>, and was staged mainly by enlisted men in the Air Force. The putsch was quickly suppressed by forces commanded by Chief of General Staff <a href="/wiki/Mahamoud_Mohamed" title="Mahamoud Mohamed">Mahamoud Mohamed</a>, a veteran Somali military official.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> They included the General Service Unit (GSU)&#8212;a paramilitary wing of the police&#8212;and later the regular police.\n</p><p>On the heels of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garissa_Massacre" title="Garissa Massacre">Garissa Massacre</a> of 1980, Kenyan troops committed the <a href="/wiki/Wagalla_massacre" title="Wagalla massacre">Wagalla massacre</a> in 1984 against thousands of civilians in <a href="/wiki/Wajir_County" title="Wajir County">Wajir County</a>. An official probe into the atrocities was later ordered in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The election held in 1988 saw the advent of the <i>mlolongo</i> (queuing) system, where voters were supposed to line up behind their favoured candidates instead of a secret ballot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> This was seen as the climax of a very undemocratic regime and it led to widespread agitation for constitutional reform. Several contentious clauses, including one that allowed for only one political party, were changed in the following years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_of_multiparty_politics_and_the_demise_of_Moi_era">Birth of multiparty politics and the demise of Moi era</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Multiparty_politics_in_Kenya&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Multiparty politics in Kenya (page does not exist)">multiparty politics in Kenya</a></div>\n<p>Kenya became a multiparty state in 1991 after 26 years of being a single party state. On 28 October 1992, president Moi dissolved parliament, five months before the end of his term. As a result, preparations began for all elective seats in parliament as well as the president. The elections were scheduled to take place on 7 December 1992, but delays led to its postponement to 29 December the same year. Apart from KANU, the ruling party, other parties represented in the elections included <a href="/wiki/Forum_for_the_Restoration_of_Democracy" title="Forum for the Restoration of Democracy">FORD</a> Kenya and FORD Asili. This election was marked by large-scale intimidation of opponents, as well as harassment of election officials. It resulted in an economic crisis propagated by ethnic violence as the president was accused of rigging electoral results to sustain power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keith_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keith-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldsworthy_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldsworthy-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParliamentKe_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-ParliamentKe-72">[72]</a></sup> This election was a turning point for Kenya as it signified the beginning of the end of Moi\'s leadership and the rule of KANU. Moi retained the presidency and <a href="/wiki/George_Saitoti" title="George Saitoti">George Saitoti</a> became the vice president. Although it retained power, KANU won 100 seats and lost 88 seats to the six opposition parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keith_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keith-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParliamentKe_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-ParliamentKe-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><b>Round no 1 (29 December 1992): Elections results</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>Tally</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Number of registered electors\n</td>\n<td>7,900,366\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Voters\n</td>\n<td>5,486,768 (69.4%)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Blank or invalid ballot papers\n</td>\n<td>61,173\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Valid votes\n</td>\n<td>5,425,595\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><b>Round no 1: Distribution of seats</b>\n</td>\n<td colspan="3">\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Political Group</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>Total</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Kenya African National Union (KANU)\n</td>\n<td>100\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Forum for the Restoration of Democracy (FORD-Kenya)\n</td>\n<td>31\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Forum for the Restoration of Democracy (FORD-Asili)\n</td>\n<td>31\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Democratic Party (DP)\n</td>\n<td>23\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Kenya Social Congress (KSC)\n</td>\n<td>1\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Kenya National Congress (KNC)\n</td>\n<td>1\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Party of independent Candidates of Kenya (PICK)\n</td>\n<td>1\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The elections of 1992 market the beginning of multiparty politics after more than 25 years rule by KANU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keith_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Keith-70">[70]</a></sup> Following the skirmishes in the aftermath of the 1992 multiparty elections, 5,000 people were killed and a further 75,000 others displaced from their homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In the next five years, many political alliances were formed in preparation for the next elections. In 1994, <a href="/wiki/Jaramogi_Oginga_Odinga" title="Jaramogi Oginga Odinga">Jaramogi Oginga Odinga</a> died and several coalitions joined his FORD Kenya party to form a new party called United National Democratic Alliance. However, this party was plagued with disagreements. In 1995, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Leakey" title="Richard Leakey">Richard Leakey</a> formed the Safina party, but it was denied registration until November 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCKenyaHIst_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCKenyaHIst-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1996, KANU revised the constitution to allow Moi to remain president for another term. Subsequently, Moi stood for re-election and won a 5th term in 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> His win was strongly critisised by his major opponents, <a href="/wiki/Mwai_Kibaki" title="Mwai Kibaki">Kibaki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raila_Odinga" title="Raila Odinga">Odinga</a> as being fraudulent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCKenyaHist_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCKenyaHist-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-77">[77]</a></sup> Following this win, Moi was constitutionally barred from vying for another presidential term at the end of his term. Beginning in 1998, Moi attempted to influence the country\'s succession politics to have <a href="/wiki/Uhuru_Kenyatta" title="Uhuru Kenyatta">Uhuru Kenyatta</a> elected in the upcoming 2002 elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="President_Kibaki_and_the_road_to_a_new_constitution">President Kibaki and the road to a new constitution</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mwai_Kibaki" title="Mwai Kibaki">Mwai Kibaki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Mwai_Kibaki" title="Presidency of Mwai Kibaki">Presidency of Mwai Kibaki</a>, <a href="/wiki/2002_Kenyan_general_election" title="2002 Kenyan general election">2002 Kenyan general election</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2007_Kenyan_general_election" title="2007 Kenyan general election">2007 Kenyan general election</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nairobi_Kibera_04.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2133" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Nairobi_Kibera_04.JPG/220px-Nairobi_Kibera_04.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Nairobi_Kibera_04.JPG/330px-Nairobi_Kibera_04.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Nairobi_Kibera_04.JPG/440px-Nairobi_Kibera_04.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nairobi_Kibera_04.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of <a href="/wiki/Kibera" title="Kibera">Kibera</a>, the largest urban <a href="/wiki/Slum" title="Slum">slum</a> in Africa</div></div></div>\n<p>Moi\'s plan to be replaced by Uhuru Kenyatta failed, and <a href="/wiki/Mwai_Kibaki" title="Mwai Kibaki">Mwai Kibaki</a>, running for the opposition coalition "National Rainbow Coalition" (<a href="/wiki/National_Rainbow_Coalition" title="National Rainbow Coalition">NARC</a>), was elected President. Anderson (2003) reports the elections were judged free and fair by local and international observers, and seemed to mark a turning point in Kenya\'s democratic evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-77">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2005, Kenyans rejected a plan to replace the 1963 independence constitution with a new one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> As a result, the elections of 2007 happened following the old constitution. Kibaki was re-elected in a highly contested political scene which was marked by <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%9308_Kenyan_crisis" title="2007&#8211;08 Kenyan crisis">political and ethnic violence</a>. This resulted from accusations by the main opposition leader, Raila Odinga, that the election results were rigged and that he was the rightfully elected president. As a result, 1,500 people were killed and another 600,000 were internally displaced, making it the worst <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_unrest" title="Civil unrest">post-election violence</a> in Kenya. To stop the death and displacement of people, Kibaki and Raila agreed to work together, with the latter taking the position of a prime minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> This made Raila  <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Kenya" title="Prime Minister of Kenya">the second prime minister of Kenya</a>.\n</p><p>In July 2010, Kenya partnered with other East African countries to form the new East African Common Market within the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In August 2010, Kenyans held a <a href="/wiki/2010_Kenyan_constitutional_referendum" title="2010 Kenyan constitutional referendum">referendum</a> and passed <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Kenya" title="Constitution of Kenya">a new constitution</a>, which limited presidential powers and devolved the central government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCKenyaHIst_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBCKenyaHIst-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Devolution_of_government_and_separation_of_powers">Devolution of government and separation of powers</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Kenya" title="Administrative divisions of Kenya">Administrative divisions of Kenya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Kenya" title="Constitution of Kenya">Constitution of Kenya</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Uhuru_Kenyatta" title="Presidency of Uhuru Kenyatta">Presidency of Uhuru Kenyatta</a></div>\n<p>Following the passage of the new constitution, Kenya became a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a><a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democratic</a> republic, whereby the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Kenya" title="President of Kenya">President of Kenya</a> is both <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a>, and of a <a href="/wiki/Multi-party_system" title="Multi-party system">multi-party system</a>. The new constitution also states that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Executive_power" title="Executive power">executive powers</a> is exercised by the executive branch of government, headed by the President, who chairs the cabinet, that is composed of people chosen from outside parliament. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legislative_power" title="Legislative power">Legislative power</a> is vested exclusively in <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Kenya" title="Parliament of Kenya">Parliament</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">judiciary</a> is independent of the executive and the legislature. Mwai Kibaki became the first president to serve under this new constitution while Uhuru Kenyatta became the first president elected under this constitution.\n</p><p>In 2011, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Linda_Nchi" title="Operation Linda Nchi">Kenya began sending troops</a> to Somalia to fight the Islamic terror group, <a href="/wiki/Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)" title="Al-Shabaab (militant group)">Al-Shabaab</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In mid-2011, two consecutive missed rainy seasons precipitated the worst <a href="/wiki/2011_East_Africa_drought" title="2011 East Africa drought">drought in East Africa</a> seen in 60 years. The northwestern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkana_District" title="Turkana District">Turkana region</a> was especially affected,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RedCross_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-RedCross-83">[83]</a></sup> with local schools shut down as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kscafthit_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kscafthit-84">[84]</a></sup> The crisis was reportedly over by early 2012 because of coordinated relief efforts. Aid agencies subsequently shifted their emphasis to recovery initiatives, including digging irrigation canals and distributing plant seeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unsfisiobrr_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Unsfisiobrr-85">[85]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2013, Kenya had its <a href="/wiki/2013_Kenyan_general_election" title="2013 Kenyan general election">first general elections</a> after the new constitution had been passed. Uhuru Kenyatta won in a disputed election result, leading to a petition by the opposition leader, Raila Odinga. The supreme court upheld the election results and <a href="/wiki/Uhuru_Kenyatta" title="Uhuru Kenyatta">President Kenyatta</a> began his term with <a href="/wiki/William_Ruto" title="William Ruto">William Ruto</a> as the deputy president. Despite the outcome of this ruling, the Supreme Court and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_Judge" title="Supreme Court Judge">head of the Supreme Court</a> were seen as a powerful institutions that could carry out its role of checking the powers of the president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2017, Uhuru Kenyatta won a second term in office in another <a href="/wiki/2017_Kenyan_general_election" title="2017 Kenyan general election">disputed election</a>. Following the defeat, Raila Odinga again petitioned the results in the Supreme Court accusing the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Independent_Electoral_and_Bundaries_commission_of_Kenya&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Independent Electoral and Bundaries commission of Kenya (page does not exist)">electoral commission</a> of mismanagement of the elections and Uhuru Kenyatta and his party of rigging. The Supreme Court overturned the election results in what became a landmark ruling in Africa and one of the very few in the world in which the results of a presidential elections were annulled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> This ruling solidified the position of the Supreme Court as an independent body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Consequently, Kenya had a second round of elections for the presidential position, in which Uhuru emerged the winner after Raila refused to participate citing irregularities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_climate">Geography and climate</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Kenya" title="Geography of Kenya">Geography of Kenya</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Un-kenya.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3092" data-file-width="2367" decoding="async" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Un-kenya.png/220px-Un-kenya.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Un-kenya.png/330px-Un-kenya.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Un-kenya.png/440px-Un-kenya.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Un-kenya.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of Kenya.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kenya_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1900" data-file-width="1900" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Kenya_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/220px-Kenya_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Kenya_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/330px-Kenya_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Kenya_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/440px-Kenya_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kenya_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="K&#246;ppen climate classification">K&#246;ppen climate classification</a> map of Kenya.</div></div></div>\n<p>At 580,367&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (224,081&#160;sq&#160;mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-cia-2">[2]</a></sup> Kenya is the world\'s forty-seventh largest country (after <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>). It lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/5th_parallel_north" title="5th parallel north">5&#176;N</a> and <a href="/wiki/5th_parallel_south" title="5th parallel south">5&#176;S</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/34th_meridian_east" title="34th meridian east">34&#176;</a> and <a href="/wiki/42nd_meridian_east" title="42nd meridian east">42&#176;E</a>. From the coast on the Indian Ocean, the low plains rise to central highlands. The highlands are bisected by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kenyan_Rift_Valley" title="Kenyan Rift Valley">Great Rift Valley</a>, with a fertile plateau lying to the east.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>The Kenyan Highlands are one of the most successful agricultural production regions in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-91">[91]</a></sup> The highlands are the site of the highest point in Kenya and the second highest peak on the continent: <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kenya" title="Mount Kenya">Mount Kenya</a>, which reaches 5,199&#160;m (17,057&#160;ft) and is the site of glaciers. <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kilimanjaro" title="Mount Kilimanjaro">Mount Kilimanjaro</a> (5,895&#160;m or 19,341&#160;ft) can be seen from Kenya to the south of the Tanzanian border.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>\n<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kenya&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2016</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Kenya\'s climate varies from tropical along the coast to temperate inland to <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> in the north and northeast parts of the country. The area receives a great deal of sunshine every month, and summer clothes are worn throughout the year. It is usually cool at night and early in the morning inland at higher elevations.\n</p><p>The "long rains" season occurs from March/April to May/June. The "short rains" season occurs from October to November/December. The rainfall is sometimes heavy and often falls in the afternoons and evenings. The temperature remains high throughout these months of tropical rain. The hottest period is February and March, leading into the season of the long rains, and the coldest is in July, until mid August.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_-_Skyline_-_Nairobi_-_Park.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Giraffe_-_Skyline_-_Nairobi_-_Park.jpg/220px-Giraffe_-_Skyline_-_Nairobi_-_Park.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Giraffe_-_Skyline_-_Nairobi_-_Park.jpg/330px-Giraffe_-_Skyline_-_Nairobi_-_Park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Giraffe_-_Skyline_-_Nairobi_-_Park.jpg/440px-Giraffe_-_Skyline_-_Nairobi_-_Park.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_-_Skyline_-_Nairobi_-_Park.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A giraffe at <a href="/wiki/Nairobi_National_Park" title="Nairobi National Park">Nairobi National Park</a>, with Nairobi\'s skyline in background</div></div></div>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:right;">\n<caption><span style="font-size:110%;">Average annual temperatures</span>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align:left;">City\n</th>\n<th>Elevation (m)</th>\n<th>Max (&#176;C)</th>\n<th>Min (&#176;C)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td
